Plus, it&#8217;s made in Italy.</p>\n</p><a href='https://club.macstories.net/?utm_source=ms&#038;utm_medium=rss'>Join Now</a>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 31 Jul 2020 16:35:46 +0000","feedId":526,"bgimg":"https://56243e3f6f46fe44a301-deabeb5f3878e3553d0b065ea974f9bf.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/256px.png","linkMd5":"bc09f00361bdfd450eac3180c48c712c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn89@2020_3/2020/08/16/11-54-46-345_901ee973c59d45b7.webp","destWidth":256,"destHeight":256,"author":"John Voorhees","enclosureType":"audio/mpeg","enclosureUrl":"https://cdn.simplecast.com/audio/0de98b/0de98bba-5e4d-4879-94dc-8c76a3a88322/1ae76d80-bea0-4b4b-8ee3-1a9abb2c4267/mac-catalyst-due-reminders-on-mac-and-a-fiery-feeds-update_tc.mp3","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-itunes-2x1495584446433.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn29@2020_6/2020/08/16/11-54-48-951_44f6e726042a0d5b.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-overcast-2x1495584446439.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn97@2020_1/2020/08/16/11-54-46-435_63962739202511e1.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-pocket-casts-2x1495584446442.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn74@2020_4/2020/08/16/11-54-49-676_683cc37726177cc0.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-rss-2x1495584446444.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn37@2020_5/2020/08/16/11-54-49-107_962140602f5518bd.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","title":"Podcasting on iPad Pro and Mac: A Streamlined Approach with the Sound Devices MixPre-3 II","link":"https://www.macstories.net/?p=63923","description":"<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02085-1595472056039.jpg\" alt=\"\"><p class=\"image-caption\"></p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p2\">Anyone new to a job has probably thought or even asked:</p> \n<blockquote id=\"blockquote3\">\n <p> “Why do you do it that way?” </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p id=\"p4\">and gotten the age-old answer:</p> \n<blockquote id=\"blockquote5\">\n <p> “Because that’s how we’ve always done it.” </p>\n</blockquote> \n<p id=\"p6\">It’s human nature to stick with a solution that works and is familiar. When a workflow is so ingrained that it’s a habit, that’s good because it allows the task to become less about the tools and more about what you’re creating.</p> \n<p id=\"p7\">Paradoxically, though, familiarity can also lead to inflexibility, a resistance to change that undermines the very productivity that the solution enabled in the first place. It’s an inertial force that’s hard to resist, but I think it’s important to push back against it. Not solely for the sake of efficiency, but also to improve the results of your efforts.</p> \n<p id=\"p8\">The trick is knowing when experimentation with new workflows is unproductive fiddling and when it’s meaningful exploration. I’ve seen too many people fall into the trap where improving the process becomes the goal itself.</p> \n<p id=\"p9\">Early last year, I decided I was finished with letting tasks dictate how I work. I work across the Mac, an iPad Pro, and an iPhone all day long. Some jobs are more suited to one device than another, and some I just prefer to do on a particular device. The point is, though, that it’s something I want to be my choice, instead of something foisted on me by the nature of the work itself.</p> \n<p id=\"p10\">I’m fortunate that most of what I do migrates effortlessly from one device to another. Still, I’ve historically had two weekly responsibilities where I’ve felt tied to a Mac.</p> \n<p id=\"p11\">The first was producing the Club MacStories newsletters using Mailchimp’s web app. <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/desktop-class-safari-for-ipad-a-hands-on-look-at-the-difference-the-ipados-update-makes-to-apples-browser/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">As I wrote last January</a>, Safari’s updates in iOS and iPadOS 13, which made web apps work roughly the same on my iPad Pro as on my Mac, solved that problem for me.</p> \n<p id=\"p12\">The second Mac-bound task was podcast recording and production. I recognize that there have been ways to accomplish podcasting on an iPad for a while. However, when it comes to recording in particular, I didn’t want to change the way I record episodes to work around the iPad’s limitations for <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/beyond-the-tablet/11/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">the same reasons Federico articulated</a> in <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/beyond-the-tablet/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Beyond the Tablet</a>.</p> \n<p id=\"p13\">What made these two tasks frustrating is that they’re both tied to schedules that have limited flexibility. When I was traveling more, that left me with little choice but to take a Mac. I prefer to travel with my iPad Pro, but regardless, I’d rather pick how I work myself, even when I’m at home.</p> \n<p id=\"p14\">I’m not doing as much traveling now, but a recent road trip to Michigan led me to start thinking about my podcasting setup again. As with many trips in the past, I wound up taking my MacBook Pro along, in this case so I could record interviews for <a href=\"https://appstories.net/episodes/174/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">an episode of AppStories</a>. The setup was perfectly fine, but it felt like too much equipment for recording a few short interviews. Plus, I took my iPad Pro because I prefer it for writing and wanted to stay connected to mobile data as I wrote a story in the car.</p> \n<p id=\"p15\">Ever since Jason Snell wrote about <a href=\"https://sixcolors.com/post/2019/02/a-week-of-podcasting-with-only-an-ipad-pro/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">his iPad Pro podcasting setup on Six Colors</a> early last year, and <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/beyond-the-tablet/11/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Federico adapted it for his setup</a> when he can’t use his Mac mini, I’ve wanted to try something similar. What held me back, though, was a combination of the complexity, cost, and infrequency with which I assumed I’d use the setup.</p> \n<p id=\"p16\">I also held out hope that iPadOS 13 or 14 would include improved audio routing that would make it possible to talk on Skype and record a local audio track. That hasn’t happened. Although I expect Apple will add that functionality eventually, it’s been 18 months since Safari solved my Mailchimp problem. With only podcasting standing in the way of my goal of device independence, and no software solution from Apple in the early iPadOS 14 betas, I figured it was time to revisit my hardware options.</p> \n<p id=\"p17\">I wanted a solution that worked equally well when I’m sitting at my Mac or iPad, allowing me to talk over Skype and record myself locally. What I discovered was an incredibly versatile solution that accomplishes in a single device what Snell and Federico cleverly constructed from a field recorder and USB audio interface: <a href=\"https://www.sounddevices.com/product/mixpre-3-ii/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">the Sound Devices MixPre-3 II</a>.</p> \n<p id=\"p18\">\n <!--more--></p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/mixpre3_ii_angle-1595472141802.png\" alt=\"The MixPre-3 II. Source: Sound Devices\"><p class=\"image-caption\">The MixPre-3 II. Source: Sound Devices</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p20\">There is more than one way to record a podcast, so it’s worth a brief aside about how we record at MacStories. As I already mentioned, Federico covers the same ground in more detail in his <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/beyond-the-tablet/11/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Beyond the Tablet</a> story, so I won’t belabor it here. However, it’s important to keep in mind that the conversation happens over Skype, and we each record our audio tracks locally. When we’re finished, Federico sends me his track, I line everything up, and edit them to produce the show. We record the Skype call as a reference track and backup, but we don’t use that audio in production. <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/beyond-the-tablet/11/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Like Federico</a>, I wanted an iPad solution that replicates what I do on the Mac to cut down on the equipment I need and ensure consistent quality from episode to episode regardless of whether I’m using a Mac or iPad.</p> \n<p id=\"p21\">The trouble with the iPad is the inability to run a Skype call and record your local audio track at the same time without some sort of additional hardware. Snell and Federico’s solutions work around this by recording to an SD card in a <a href=\"https://www.zoom-na.com/products/field-video-recording/field-recording/zoom-h6-handy-recorder-1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Zoom H6</a> field recorder, routing the audio from the H6 to a <a href=\"https://www.sounddevices.com/product/usbpre-2/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Sound Devices USBPre 2 audio interface</a>, which handles the Skype call on the iPad Pro, so everyone can hear each other. What’s cool about the MixPre-3 II is that it replicates the same setup in a single, smaller device that costs less and has a few additional features that make it easy to use.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02068-1595472273801.jpg\" alt=\"One advantage of my MixPre-3 II setup is that it's nearly identical on the Mac and iPad.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">One advantage of my MixPre-3 II setup is that it’s nearly identical on the Mac and iPad.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p23\">It’s not surprising that the solution to what I was looking for is hardware made by Sound Devices. After all, Snell and Federico’s approaches incorporate the company’s USBPre 2 audio interface, which is a terrific piece of equipment. Due to the limitations of iPadOS, though, the USBPre 2 isn’t enough on its own. Snell’s insight that led him to add a Zoom H6 recorder to the mix captures the difference between the MixPre-3 II and the USBPre 2.</p> \n<p id=\"p24\">When I came across the MixPre-3 II, I quickly realized that I couldn’t be sure if the MixPre would suit my needs until I tried it. That’s because there doesn’t seem to be anyone using gear like the MixPre with the iPad Pro and writing about it. Fortunately, Amazon has a generous return policy, so I decided to see if I could figure it out for myself.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/Untitled-1595472511897.png\" alt=\"The MixPre-3 II includes a solid set of inputs on its left and right sides. Source: Sound Devices.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">The MixPre-3 II includes a solid set of inputs on its left and right sides. Source: Sound Devices.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p26\">The MixPre-3 II is an incredibly versatile device, which makes it inherently complex. However, most of the complexity is tucked away in the device’s menu system, accessible from a tiny touchscreen on its front, and once it’s set up, you can ignore the complexity. In addition to the touchscreen, the face of the MixPre-3 II includes three satisfyingly metal knobs for each channel, plus play, stop, and record buttons. The left side of the MixPre-3 has two XLR microphone inputs, an on/off switch which is too hard to access, USB-A and USB-C ports, and a 3.5&nbsp;mm stereo out jack. On the right is another XLR input, a 3.5&nbsp;mm headphone jack, a 3.5&nbsp;mm Aux/Mic In jack, a micro HDMI port that records codes from a connected camera, and a dial that serves as a volume control for the headphones and an alternate means of navigating the touchscreen UI. The back of the device houses a battery pack that can be used to power the MixPre, and behind it is a slot for an SD card to capture recordings.</p> \n<p id=\"p27\">When the MixPre-3 II arrived, I immediately set it up using my <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B004QJOZS4/ref=cm_sw_r_cp_tai_YmogFbM19K1C6\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Audio Technica ATR2100-USB microphone</a> that I use when I travel, connecting an XLR cable to channel 1 on the MixPre. I grabbed some headphones, connected those, and tethered the MixPre to my iPad Pro using the USB-C to USB-C cable that came with it. What followed was several frustrating hours of experimentation.</p> \n<p id=\"p28\">The trouble was that, although I could record my audio locally to the SD card, the audio of the Skype call still played over my iPad’s speakers and used the iPad’s microphone. It was a perplexing problem because audio from other apps was properly routed over the USB-C cable.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02091-1595472673226.jpg\" alt=\"The solution for Skype was to route its audio from the USB-C port on the iPad Pro to the MixPre-3 II's Aux/Mic In port.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">The solution for Skype was to route its audio from the USB-C port on the iPad Pro to the MixPre-3 II’s Aux/Mic In port.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p30\">The solution was to disconnect the USB-C cable from the iPad Pro and use <a href=\"https://www.apple.com/shop/product/MU7E2AM/A/usb-c-to-35-mm-headphone-jack-adapter\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">the USB-C to 3.5&nbsp;mm adapter</a> Apple sells for wired headphones plus a 3.5&nbsp;mm to 3.5&nbsp;mm cable from the adapter to the Aux/Mic In jack on the MixPre-3. That properly routed the Skype call, including my voice, to my headphones. This solution comes with one pretty significant caveat. Although the audio is routed over the 3.5&nbsp;mm cable, Skype still uses the internal iPad microphone. The sound quality of conducting a Skype call this way is acceptable. However, it means that if the local track recorded by your external microphone fails, your only fallback is a Skype call where you’ve been recorded over the iPad’s internal microphone, which will not sound as good as it would if your external microphone was used for the Skype call too. Whether this is a Skype, MixPre, or iPadOS limitation isn’t entirely clear, but it’s worth keeping in mind.</p> \n<p id=\"p31\">There are several ways to power the MixPre, including its USB-C port and the battery sled that attaches to the device’s back and can take AA batteries or Sony L-Mount batteries. The MixPre needs 7.5W to operate, so it can be powered by the iPad Pro’s USB-C port, which delivers the necessary power. However, because I’m using the iPad’s USB-C port to route audio to the MixPre, I’ve been powering the MixPre with a USB-C cable connected to a rechargeable battery pack or plugged into the wall with the AC adapter that comes with the MixPre.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02076-1595472792795.jpg\" alt=\"Unlike the iPad Pro, a USB-C cable connected to my Mac mini handles power and audio routing.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Unlike the iPad Pro, a USB-C cable connected to my Mac mini handles power and audio routing.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p33\">The setup is nearly the same when I’m at my Mac, except in that mode, I attach the USB-C cable to my Mac mini. Because the Mac doesn’t have the same audio limitations as the iPad, I can use Skype’s Mac app to conduct the call and <a href=\"https://rogueamoeba.com/audiohijack/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Audio Hijack</a> to record my audio just as I’ve always done.</p> \n<p id=\"p34\">I have only recorded a couple of podcast episodes with this setup so far, but I like it a lot already. The hardware is solidly built, and the touchscreen is bright, showing off each track’s levels as I record. Another nice touch is that the channel knobs are ringed with LEDs too, which provide a second visual indicator of your levels.</p> \n<p id=\"p35\">However, one of the biggest advantages is just how little needs to be changed to move from the Mac to my iPad. The USB-C cable needs to be connected to a power source, and I need to add the 3.5&nbsp;mm cable to the Aux/Mic In jack, but that’s it.</p> \n<p id=\"p36\">Because the MixPre handles three separate channels, the adjustments I need to make there are minimal too. I dedicated channel 1 to my microphone, which needs a couple of tweaks when switching between my <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0002BACBO/ref=cm_sw_r_cp_tai_VwogFbJ2ZVVST\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Shure Beta 87A</a> and Audio Technica ATR2100-USB microphones. Channel 2 handles audio coming into the Aux/Mic In jack when I’m recording on my iPad, and channel 3 handles audio coming over USB-C when I’m working on my Mac. Whichever channel I’m not using can be turned off. Better yet, I can save my Mac and iPad settings as tiny files that sit on my SD card, where they can be reinstalled if things get messed up.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/mixpre3_ii_flatlay_16x9-1595472953435.jpg\" alt=\"The MixPre-3 II isn't cheap, but it has advantages over other solutions. Source: Sound Devices.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">The MixPre-3 II isn’t cheap, but it has advantages over other solutions. Source: Sound Devices.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p38\">The hybrid solution developed by Snell and Federico took advantage of hardware they already owned, which is perfectly understandable, but purpose-built hardware like the MixPre has several advantages. Two are size and weight, which is important if you’re using an iPad Pro to record on the go. The USBPre 2 is 180 x 100 x 43&nbsp;mm in size and weighs 500 grams. The MixPre-3 II is 144 x 110 x 36&nbsp;mm and the same weight as the USBPre 2, but about 33% smaller by volume. To provide additional context, the MixPre has about the same footprint as a Magic Trackpad and thickness of an Apple TV. When you add the Zoom H6 to the mix, which is 77.8 x 152.8 x 47.8&nbsp;mm and 280 grams, the entire package becomes substantially bigger and heavier than the MixPre alone.</p> \n<p id=\"p39\">Another issue is cost. The MixPre-3 II isn’t cheap at $680. However, it’s nearly half the price of a USBPre 2 and Zoom H6 together. You’re still paying a premium price for pro-level gear, but in the world of high-end audio equipment of this quality and versatility, $680 is a fair price.</p> \n<p id=\"p40\">I’ve discovered a few other advantages to the MixPre that I didn’t anticipate too. The first is that the device records poly WAV files to its SD card. That means it can record multiple channels to a single WAV file that you can later split into separate tracks for editing. This isn’t limited to the usual left and right channels used for stereo sound. I can record the full Skype audio, my local audio, and a track that isolates Federico’s audio simultaneously in both configurations. Federico’s audio is still coming over Skype, so the quality isn’t as good as his locally recorded track. However, if his recording ever fails, having a separate track with just his voice will be an advantage when editing.</p> \n<p id=\"p41\">When I record on my Mac, I record to its internal SSD using Audio Hijack. What the MixPre gives me that I didn’t have with the <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B00MIXF2RS/ref=cm_sw_r_cp_tai_vCogFbXH4CHAM\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Tascam US-2x2</a> that I used before is the ability to record on an SD card too. What’s nice about that is it’s a backup that’s completely separate from my Mac’s hardware in the event the Mac catastrophically fails.</p> \n<p id=\"p42\">The backup situation with the iPad isn’t quite as robust, but it’s better than I expected. The MixPre has a USB-A port to which you can attach a small USB thumb drive or even a powered external drive.<sup id=\"fnref-63923-1\"><a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=63923#fn-63923-1\" class=\"jetpack-footnote\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">1</a></sup> As soon as you stop recording on the MixPre, the record button begins to flash, indicating that the file saved to the SD card is being automatically backed up to the thumb drive, while the progress is shown on the MixPre’s display. Having a second copy even if it’s not created simultaneously as you record provides additional protection. Because it’s automatic, you won’t forget to do it either, provided you leave the thumb drive plugged into the MixPre.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/PNG%20image-6C6118389212-1-1595505174632.png\" alt=\"When I'm finished recording, I can switch the USB-C cable from powering the MixPre-3 II to the iPad Pro's USB-C port for transferring files, eliminating the need for an SD card reader.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">When I’m finished recording, I can switch the USB-C cable from powering the MixPre-3 II to the iPad Pro’s USB-C port for transferring files, eliminating the need for an SD card reader.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p44\">I also love that the MixPre can act as a mass storage device. From the System menu on the device, tapping File Transfer switches the MixPre to storage mode. In this mode, moving the USB-C cable from powering the MixPre to plugged into the iPad Pro is a quick way to copy files from the SD card to the iPad without needing to open the battery compartment and remove the SD card. It’s both a convenience that avoids the need for an SD card reader and another way to quickly offload recordings for safekeeping in the cloud and locally on your iPad.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/iVBORw0KGgoAAAANSUhEUgAAC50AAAjwCAYAAAAKgq9xAAAACXBIWXMAAAsTAAALEwEAmpwYAAAM-2%2020-1595505184684.PNG\" alt=\"Dragging files from the MixPre's SD card to a folder in iCloud Drive for processing.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Dragging files from the MixPre’s SD card to a folder in iCloud Drive for processing.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p46\">Sound Devices also makes an iOS and iPadOS app called <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/sound-devices-wingman/id1137249438\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Wingman</a> that works with the MixPre using Bluetooth. The app is in serious need of some design love, but it works well. The app allows me to start and stop recordings, monitor levels, edit file metadata, which is much nicer with the iOS keyboard than the MixPre’s touchscreen, and manage recordings. Especially with a new device like this, I’ve appreciated the ability to prop up my iPad mini nearby while keeping an eye on levels and making small adjustments.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/IMG_0155%202-1595505492118.PNG\" alt=\"Monitoring levels on my iPad mini using Sound Devices' Wingman app.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Monitoring levels on my iPad mini using Sound Devices’ Wingman app.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p48\">It’s fairly common for field recorders, but the MixPre comes with tripod connection points on the unit’s top and bottom too. That allows you to connect a camera when you’re shooting video and using an external microphone. However, simpler alternatives work too. I’m using <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B00D76RNLS/ref=cm_sw_r_cp_tai_9IogFb59V0MMQ\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">a small tabletop tripod by Manfrotto</a> to elevate the MixPre off my desk and tilt it up a little, making the display easier to read and creating a <a href=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02068-1595472273801.jpg\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">shelter for Pikachu</a>.</p> \n<p id=\"p49\">With a hardware solution to recording locally and monitoring a Skype call, the only other piece of the iPad Pro podcasting puzzle is editing, which is straightforward. My editing process involves file preprocessing for loudness matching and cleaning up audio. I use Mac tools for that step of the process, but they are easily accessed by saving remotely-recorded tracks to iCloud Drive and then opening them by accessing my Mac mini from anywhere with <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/screens/id655890150\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Screens</a>. I wouldn’t want to edit that way, but preprocessing requires me to open the files and click only a few buttons to start the processing.</p> \n<p id=\"p50\">For editing on the go, I’ve been experimenting with <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/ferrite-recording-studio/id1018780185\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Ferrite</a>. I haven’t produced a released episode with Ferrite yet, but I’ve experimented with it enough to be comfortable that I can. It took a while to find my way around the app. Still, between <a href=\"https://youtu.be/675gW3a0IAc\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Jason Snell’s excellent video</a> demonstrating how he edits using the Apple Pencil, the app’s terrific help system, and finding the keyboard shortcuts I needed, Ferrite is ultimately easier to use than <a href=\"https://www.apple.com/logic-pro/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Logic Pro X</a>. I like Logic a lot, but it does so much more than the subset of tools I use that Ferrite is a refreshing change. I just wish it existed on the Mac too, so I could edit in similar ways on the iPad Pro and Mac.</p> \n<hr id=\"hr51\">\n <p id=\"p52\"><a href=\"https://www.ruminatepodcast.com/episodes/1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">I started podcasting in 2015</a> and used a Tascam USB 2x2 for the last four years. It has served me well, but it was never a device that was designed for travel. The Tascam is just a little too big for that. When I traveled, I used a <a href=\"https://www.zoom-na.com/products/field-video-recording/field-recording/h5-handy-recorder\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Zoom H5</a> field recorder, but I didn’t use the Zoom regularly, which meant I always had to refresh my memory on how to use it before a trip. The Zoom’s monochrome display is also difficult to read compared to the MixPre.</p> \n <p id=\"p53\">In contrast, I’ll use the MixPre to record 2-3 shows per week, regardless of whether I’m in front of my Mac. That means its menus and settings will become second nature quickly, making recording on my iPad Pro just as effortless and comfortable. That alone makes the MixPre-3 II worth its price to me. The MixPre-3 II liberates podcast recording from a single device, allowing me to choose how I want to record instead of having how I work dictated by the task at hand.</p> \n <div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n  <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/DSC02075-1595473255885.jpg\" alt=\"\"><p class=\"image-caption\"></p></img>\n </div> \n <p id=\"p55\">Still, I don’t expect to start recording our shows all over my house or in the backyard. There remains an advantage to recording in a controlled environment sitting in front of my 27-inch display. That isn’t going to change. The point is not to escape from the Mac’s clutches; it’s freeing me up to choose how I work and providing more options when recording at my Mac isn’t possible.</p> \n <p id=\"p56\">If you’d asked me a month ago why my podcasting setup and workflow was the way it was, my answer wouldn’t have been very far from “Because that’s how I’ve always done it.” Fortunately, my awkward recording setup while vacationing rekindled my quest for a device-independent workflow that started last year.</p> \n <p id=\"p57\">The difficulty and expense of solving a problem to work around an iPadOS limitation show that Apple still has work to do to support creative professionals on the iPad. For many people, the MixPre-3 II is probably not worth the cost. But for me, the MixPre is worth it because podcasting is a big part of the work I do each week, and I was no longer willing to wait for a software solution.</p> \n <p id=\"p58\">Workarounds are inherently frustrating. I spent several hours experimenting and studying Sound Devices’ documentation before I was content that the MixPre-3 II would work the way I wanted. I expect Apple will eventually get to iPadOS limitations like the inability to record locally while conducting a VoIP call. After all, it’s the sort of involved production workflow the company seems to want the iPad to be able to address. Until then, though, the MixPre-3 II is an excellent solution to recording podcasts on an iPad Pro if you record the way we do here at MacStories.</p> \n <div class=\"footnotes\" id=\"div59\"> \n  <hr>\n   <ol>\n    <li id=\"fn-63923-1\"> Keyboards and audio control surfaces can also be connected to the MixPre’s USB-A port.&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=63923#fnref-63923-1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">↩︎</a> </li> \n   </ol>\n  </hr>\n </div> \n <hr />\n <h3>Support MacStories Directly</h3>\n <p><p>Club MacStories offers exclusive access to <strong>extra MacStories content</strong>, delivered every week; it’s also a way to support us directly.</p> <p>Club MacStories will help you discover the best apps for your devices and get the most out of your iPhone, iPad, and Mac. Voorhees","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-itunes-2x1495584446433.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn29@2020_6/2020/08/16/11-54-48-951_44f6e726042a0d5b.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-overcast-2x1495584446439.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn97@2020_1/2020/08/16/11-54-46-435_63962739202511e1.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-pocket-casts-2x1495584446442.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn74@2020_4/2020/08/16/11-54-49-676_683cc37726177cc0.webp","https://31468fb5fb2bdd537aee-cb22f1c32609fa6d332a03da76e227e5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/button-rss-2x1495584446444.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn37@2020_5/2020/08/16/11-54-49-107_962140602f5518bd.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","title":"The Wallpaper App Review: Endless Wallpapers Tailored for Apple Devices","link":"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64012","description":"<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/5c1078c9-323b-4af0-a750-e28b9a0540b8.png\" alt=\"\"><p class=\"image-caption\"></p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p2\">I’m very picky about wallpapers on my devices, and when I finally find one I like, I stick with it for a very long time. At some point during that very long time, I start getting tired of my wallpaper and look for a replacement, only to quickly give up and conclude that none of the other options are good. The number of wallpapers available on the web is practically infinite, yet these days I scarcely bother to look for anything new.</p> \n<p id=\"p3\">So in some respects I’m both the best and worst person to review <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/id1518228413\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">The Wallpaper App</a>, a new Lumen Digital utility for iPhone, iPad, and Mac that does nothing but supply new wallpaper options. Best because I could use a new wallpaper solution, and worst because my passionate condemnation of most wallpaper options makes me inclined to find little of value in a new wallpaper app.</p> \n<p id=\"p4\">I see four primary strengths to The Wallpaper App, all of which give it an advantage over other wallpaper apps or services I’ve tried in the past.</p> \n<ol id=\"ol5\">\n <li>It’s extremely simple to navigate</li> \n <li>All wallpapers are designed to work well behind app icons, widgets, etc.<sup id=\"fnref-64012-1\"><a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64012#fn-64012-1\" class=\"jetpack-footnote\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">1</a></sup></li> \n <li>You can customize wallpaper colors manually</li> \n <li>Wallpaper size options are tailored for Apple device sizes</li> \n</ol>\n<p id=\"p6\">\n <!--more--></p> \n<p id=\"p7\">Navigation is this easy: swipe on-screen to switch between styles of wallpaper, or tap on-screen to switch between color options for the current style.<sup id=\"fnref-64012-2\"><a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64012#fn-64012-2\" class=\"jetpack-footnote\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">2</a></sup> When you find a wallpaper you like, hit the big Save button at the bottom of the screen. That’s pretty much it.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/72286b5b-b5ad-4df1-bda6-e4a6620b7a08.png\" alt=\"A handful of wallpaper style options.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">A handful of wallpaper style options.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p9\">If the app had the number of options found in most wallpaper apps, such simple navigation almost certainly wouldn’t work. But The Wallpaper App offers a purposely limited 15 styles of wallpaper. Several of these styles are modeled after Apple’s built-in wallpapers, like Ribbons, Fourteen, and Hello Sur. Every style has a distinct artistic flair to it, and importantly works well in the background of your Home screen or desktop, behind app icons, files and folders, widgets, shortcuts – all the things that keep your devices highly functional. The web is full of gorgeous wallpapers, but so many of them demand that they not be covered up. Not so the options found here, which look great in the background.</p> \n<p id=\"p10\">The small selection of style options isn’t a problem because the variations of each style are unlimited. The Wallpaper App generates new variations randomly in code, each of which feature different colors and details. So if you keep tapping forward, you’ll continue seeing more new variations. You can also swipe up from the bottom-center of the screen to toggle between light and dark versions of the same iteration or adjust its colors – now Apple just needs to support setting different wallpapers per system appearance in the OS.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/fea79c8f-9e10-4428-90de-2767017a9cc3.png\" alt=\"Every wallpaper can generate dark or light versions, or be color-adjusted.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Every wallpaper can generate dark or light versions, or be color-adjusted.</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p12\">A unique bonus feature of sorts – and I call it a bonus because the UI doesn’t explain anywhere that it exists – is that on compatible platforms you can drag and drop colors from an outside source to use them in any given wallpaper. This functionality pairs perfectly with <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/pastel/id413897608?uo=4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Pastel</a>, the color palette utility <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/reviews/pastel-review-a-modern-color-utility-for-ipad-and-iphone/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">I reviewed recently</a>. On my iPad I can keep Pastel in a Slide Over window, or in Split View next to The Wallpaper App, and pick up a color from Pastel then drop it on a given wallpaper to see it dynamically adopt the new color. It’s a very nice extra touch.</p> \n<div class=\"media-wrapper\">\n <img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/155e0734-5de5-4d57-b87f-594ca782bad7.png\" alt=\"Dropping a color from Pastel into The Wallpaper App (before and after).\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Dropping a color from Pastel into The Wallpaper App (before and after).</p></img>\n</div> \n<p id=\"p14\">One of my main pet peeves when searching for wallpapers online is that it’s hard to find ones that perfectly match my device’s screen dimensions. But since The Wallpaper App was designed first and foremost with Apple users in mind, that’s not the case here. The app knows your device dimensions, and even your current device orientation – whether portrait or landscape – so tapping Save provides a wallpaper perfectly suited for those settings. It just works, and if you want a different sized version, you can simply tap and hold the Save button to see a context menu with additional options – including one for generating Apple Watch-optimized wallpaper.</p> \n<hr id=\"hr15\">\n <p id=\"p16\">Plenty of wallpaper apps are nicely designed, but none have ever managed to meet my needs like The Wallpaper App, and as a result I’ve never kept another wallpaper app saved on my devices for long. This time is different.</p> \n <p id=\"p17\">The wallpaper styles available here closely model the strengths of Apple’s own built-in wallpaper options, and as a result I can see myself returning to The Wallpaper App every time I get the itch for a new background – not only returning to it, but also finding something new that actually works for me. That’s never been true with another app.</p> \n <p id=\"p18\">A great iPhone or iPad wallpaper, to me at least, needs to be not just beautiful but also a good complement to the things sitting on top of it – apps and widgets. The Wallpaper App gets that. If you’ve struggled finding good wallpapers for your devices, this app is well worth a try.</p> \n <p id=\"p19\">The Wallpaper App is <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/id1518228413\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">available as a free download on the App Store</a> across iPhone, iPad, and Mac.</p> \n <div class=\"footnotes\" id=\"div20\"> \n  <hr>\n   <ol>\n    <li id=\"fn-64012-1\"> Unlike 90% of wallpapers on the web, which look great as stand-alone images but are too distracting and/or obscured when used on an iPhone or iPad.&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64012#fnref-64012-1\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">↩︎</a> </li> \n    <li id=\"fn-64012-2\"> On iPhone tapping is complemented by a nice bump of haptic feedback.&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64012#fnref-64012-2\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">↩︎</a> </li> \n   </ol>\n  </hr>\n </div> \n <hr />\n <h3>Support MacStories Directly</h3>\n <p><p>Club MacStories offers exclusive access to <strong>extra MacStories content</strong>, delivered every week; it’s also a way to support us directly.</p> <p>Club MacStories will help you discover the best apps for your devices and get the most out of your iPhone, iPad, and Mac. Once your work is final, export and share standard, high resolution, and vector file types for flexible work between teams and apps.</p>\n<p id=\"p6\">The app comes free as a basic sketching tool, with the ability to unlock 200+ libraries of brushes, objects, and services via subscription or one-time purchase. To learn more about how Concepts can help you explore your ideas, visit&#160;<a href=\"https://concepts.app/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">concepts.app</a>.</p>\n<p id=\"p8\"><em>Our thanks to Concepts for sponsoring MacStories this week.</em></p>\n<hr /><h3>Support MacStories Directly</h3><p><p>Club MacStories offers exclusive access to <strong>extra MacStories content</strong>, delivered every week;  it&#8217;s also a way to support us directly.</p>\n<p>Club MacStories will help you discover the best apps for your devices and get the most out of your iPhone, iPad, and Mac. Plus, it&#8217;s made in Italy.</p>\n</p><a href='https://club.macstories.net/?utm_source=ms&#038;utm_medium=rss'>Join Now</a>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 27 Jul 2020 11:18:32 +0000","feedId":526,"bgimg":"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/concepts-5-1595696181148.4-hero.png","linkMd5":"89b7ce3fb351383064e814ce365238ea","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn29@2020_3/2020/08/16/11-54-48-631_de87897be2a62db5.webp","destWidth":1450,"destHeight":1037,"author":"John Voorhees","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/concepts-5-1595696181148.4-hero.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn81@2020_3/2020/08/16/11-54-46-743_de87897be2a62db5.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","title":"Dark Noise 2 Review: Sound Mixing, New Noises, iPad Cursor Support, and More","link":"https://www.macstories.net/?p=63885","description":"<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/ed7a803d-c57d-46e7-b664-225936ce4ff3.png\" alt=\"\"><p class=\"image-caption\"></p></div>\n<p id=\"p2\">Last summer developer Charlie Chapman launched <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/dark-noise/id1465439395?uo=4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Dark Noise</a>, an ambient noise app for iPhone and iPad that&#8217;s packed with delightful design details and key system integrations. My <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/reviews/dark-noise-review-ambient-noise-never-looked-so-good/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">initial review</a> praised how Chapman was able to take an otherwise standard utility app and build a standout experience which, from day one, raised itself above a crowded market of competitors.</p>\n<p id=\"p3\">The main reasons I loved Dark Noise from the start had little to do with the app&#8217;s basic utility: playing ambient noise. The app has always had a good selection of noise options, but nothing in the sound department made it truly special to me. It was the other areas of the app experience, everything surrounding that basic utility of ambient noise playing, that was so great. Icon animations, buttery smooth panel transitions, haptic feedback, strong iPad support, alternate app icons, Shortcuts support, a customizable widget, and more all made for a compelling product.</p>\n<p id=\"p4\">One of my only disappointments with Dark Noise was the inability to mix different noises together. Each noise could only be played in isolation, never more than one at a time, which proved a disadvantage compared to other ambient noise apps I&#8217;d used that enabled mixing different noises to create a custom soundscape. I&#8217;m happy to say that Dark Noise 2, released today, adds this functionality at last.</p>\n<p id=\"p5\">In Dark Noise 2 not only can you mix different sounds to create custom noises, but there are also eight new sound options to choose from, iCloud sync has been added for syncing your favorite sounds and custom mixes, and there&#8217;s optimized support for the iPadOS cursor. It&#8217;s a big release that retains the design elegance Dark Noise has had from day one, but expands the app&#8217;s usefulness in key ways.</p>\n<p id=\"p6\"><!--more--></p>\n<h2 id=\"sound-mixing\">Sound Mixing</h2>\n<p id=\"p7\">In any other app, adding a sound mixing feature could have been as bare-bones as letting you tap multiple sounds to cause them to play simultaneously. That would have been <em>fine</em>, but in fitting with the high bar set by the rest of the app, Chapman threw a lot of care and polish into Dark Noise&#8217;s sound mixing feature.</p>\n<p id=\"p8\">To get started creating a sound mix, simply tap the plus button in the top-right corner of the sound list. From here, you&#8217;re asked to set the mix&#8217;s name, add noises to it, and optionally customize its icon.</p>\n<p id=\"p9\">If there&#8217;s a limit to the number of sounds you can add to a custom mix, I haven&#8217;t been able to reach it yet in my testing, but I know you can add at least 10. You can even add multiple versions of the same sound for an interesting mix.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/5d64cdc1-527a-4546-a637-08239dde5a63.png\" alt=\"Creating a custom mix on iPad.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Creating a custom mix on iPad.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p11\">After each sound is added, you&#8217;ll see a volume slider accompanying it so you can tweak the levels of different noises to your liking. As you make various adjustments, there&#8217;s a play button at the bottom of the screen which enables quickly sampling the product of your work. You only need to hit play once, because after the mix kicks off you can make adjustments and have the sound update in real-time to your changes, whether you&#8217;re simply tweaking volume levels or adding or removing entire noises. The whole mix creation process is simple and intuitive.</p>\n<p id=\"p12\">When you&#8217;ve assembled the right collection of noises and sound levels for your mix, you can either save the mix and be done, or you can keep the creative juices flowing by customizing your mix&#8217;s icon. Every mix has a default icon auto-assigned, but if you tap the Edit button beneath that icon you can either upload a custom image or even a GIF to serve as the mix&#8217;s icon, or take one of Dark Noise&#8217;s existing icons as your foundation and tweak it with different colors to suit your preferences.</p>\n<p id=\"p13\">Images and GIFs are pulled directly from your Photos library, and GIFs in particular make great mix icons because they will animate when a mix is being played, just like any of the default noise icons in the app. If you want to use a built-in icon for your mix, you can choose from all the app&#8217;s options and change both the foreground and background colors to make it stand out from all other sounds in the noise list.</p>\n<p id=\"p14\">Saved mixes go into a new My Mixes section of the noise list, but you can also favorite them just like any other noise to send them to the top of the list. If you ever want to edit a custom mix, you can do so via the Edit button that appears when long-pressing or right-clicking the mix.</p>\n<h2 id=\"new-sounds\">New Sounds</h2>\n<p id=\"p15\">Dark Noise already had a great selection of sound options, but the new ones are nice additions:</p>\n<ul id=\"ul16\"><li>Rain on Tent</li>\n<li>Wind Chimes</li>\n<li>Windy Trees</li>\n<li>Seagulls</li>\n<li>Lake</li>\n<li>Ship Deck</li>\n<li>Flag</li>\n<li>Lullaby</li>\n</ul><p id=\"p17\">Many of these became instant favorites upon my first use, and of course each includes a beautiful animated icon upon being played. While I never felt like Dark Noise was lacking in sound options before, I appreciate the expanded roster.</p>\n<h2 id=\"all-the-rest\">All the Rest</h2>\n<p id=\"p18\"><strong>iPad cursor support.</strong> Before Dark Noise 2 you could still use a connected mouse or trackpad in the iPad version of the app, but nothing was optimized for that mode of input. Now, the cursor will snap to different interface elements like play/pause controls and buttons for AirPlay, sleep timers, and more. The heart glyph for favoriting sounds and the drag lines for rearranging favorites now subtly jump forward when you hover over them with the cursor too, which is a nice touch.</p>\n<p id=\"p19\"><strong>New app icons.</strong> Dark Noise already offered one of the most impressive arrays of alternate app icons, but it adds two more in this release: a skeuomorphic option inspired by macOS Big Sur&#8217;s new icon design and one that&#8217;s based on the artwork of Chapman&#8217;s own podcast, where he interviews indie developers: <a href=\"https://launchedfm.com/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Launched</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/4bb5d464-9494-4096-a630-35e42c45e348.png\" alt=\"There&#8217;s an icon for everyone.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">There&#8217;s an icon for everyone.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p21\"><strong>iCloud sync.</strong> Until now Dark Noise didn&#8217;t have a syncing system in place for keeping data between iPhone and iPad versions up to date. This honestly wasn&#8217;t a real problem, because the only thing sync would have been useful for was having your favorited sounds stay the same on both devices. It didn&#8217;t take much work to manually re-favorite the same sounds on each platform.</p>\n<p id=\"p22\">With the addition of custom sound mixes, however, it&#8217;s more important than ever to have a sync system in place, so I&#8217;m glad to see that iCloud sync is now employed both for populating your mixes across multiple platforms as well as syncing your favorites list.</p>\n<hr id=\"hr23\"><p id=\"p24\">Dark Noise 2 is a fantastic update that retains the app&#8217;s fundamental design strengths while adding valuable new functionality via custom mixes and great new sounds. I love that mix creation is a full-featured, intuitive experience rather than something that was quickly thrown together &#8211; it fits well with the whole app&#8217;s design ethos.</p>\n<p id=\"p25\">Some 2.0 releases for apps are more radical, but Dark Noise didn&#8217;t need that kind of update; rather, this latest version retains the strong foundation of the original while building upon it to offer more of what users want. If you were already a Dark Noise user, you&#8217;ll love the app even more now, and if you weren&#8217;t, there&#8217;s never been a better time to try it.</p>\n<p id=\"p26\">Dark Noise is <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/dark-noise/id1465439395?uo=4\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">available on the App Store</a> as a one-time purchase.</p>\n<hr /><h3>Support MacStories Directly</h3><p><p>Club MacStories offers exclusive access to <strong>extra MacStories content</strong>, delivered every week;  it&#8217;s also a way to support us directly.</p>\n<p>Club MacStories will help you discover the best apps for your devices and get the most out of your iPhone, iPad, and Mac. Plus, it&#8217;s made in Italy.</p>\n</p><a href='https://club.macstories.net/?utm_source=ms&#038;utm_medium=rss'>Join Now</a>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 20 Jul 2020 13:00:18 +0000","feedId":526,"bgimg":"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/ed7a803d-c57d-46e7-b664-225936ce4ff3.png","linkMd5":"27e9a8b0cb1a1b359e362548c8a4f3ca","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn26@2020_4/2020/08/16/11-54-47-320_11c467f76f293963.webp","destWidth":4095,"destHeight":2616,"author":"Ryan Christoffel","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/ed7a803d-c57d-46e7-b664-225936ce4ff3.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn58@2020_1/2020/08/16/11-54-50-167_11c467f76f293963.webp","https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/5d64cdc1-527a-4546-a637-08239dde5a63.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn33@2020_6/2020/08/16/11-54-52-873_037c8486a14c5839.webp","https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/4bb5d464-9494-4096-a630-35e42c45e348.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx17/cdn77@2020_4/2020/08/16/11-54-50-230_3a05f957351dd270.webp"}},{"createdTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","updatedTime":"2020-08-16 19:54:40","title":"Apple Maps in 2020: Cycling and EV Routing, City Guides, and Feature Parity on All Platforms","link":"https://www.macstories.net/?p=64057","description":"<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/5e2e5d0f-f25d-4659-aa19-dd985d6442d7.png\" alt=\"\"><p class=\"image-caption\"></p></div>\n<p id=\"p2\">Apple Maps has come a long way since its debut in iOS 6. Much of the app&#8217;s history, which got off to a rocky start, has been focused on gathering and improving map data worldwide, but that&#8217;s beginning to change. The task is enormous and will never really be finished, but a shift has already started.</p>\n<p id=\"p3\">Once, the improvements in Maps were focused mainly on its basic underlying data like getting roads and geographic features correct. However, today the emphasis is increasingly on providing a deeper set of data and new features like cycling routes and city guides. Google Maps has had some of this functionality for years, and many of the refinements to Apple Maps are in just a handful of cities and countries. However, with the completion of Maps&#8217; rebuilt map data in the US, Apple has begun to layer in new data and functionality that is poised to spread out much more widely.</p>\n<aside class=\"aside aligncenter aside-narrow\" id=\"aside4\"><h3 class=\"pre-title\" id=\"supported-by\">Supported By</h3>\n<h2 id=\"concepts\">Concepts</h2>\n\n<p></p><center><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/icon-1595254554207.png\" alt=\"\"></center>\n<p><strong><a href=\"https://concepts.app/download?utm_source=macstories)\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer noopener noreferrer\">Concepts</a></strong>: Sketch, Note, Draw</p>\n</aside><p id=\"p5\"><!--more--></p>\n<h2 id=\"laying-the-foundation-for-new-features-with-data\">Laying the Foundation for New Features with Data</h2>\n<p id=\"p6\">The foundation of the new features being added to Maps is the underlying data that Apple is continuously collecting and updating. The ambitious map reconstruction was <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/linked/apple-is-rebooting-its-maps-app-with-rebuilt-map-data/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">announced in June 2018</a>. A year into the project, Apple announced iOS and iPadOS 13, which <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/apple-maps-in-ios-13-sights-set-on-google/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">introduced the first new Maps feature</a> tied to the new maps: Look Around, the company&#8217;s take on Google Maps&#8217; Street View functionality. Apple has also continued to add and update data to its maps like real-time transit data, flight status information, EV charging stations, parking details, COVID-19 test centers, and indoor maps.</p>\n<p id=\"p7\">In January of this year, Apple <a href=\"https://www.justinobeirne.com/new-apple-maps-continental-united-states\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">completed its map update</a> for the continental US. In the months since, the company has continued to expand Look Around, adding US cities like <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/linked/apple-maps-look-around-feature-expands-to-chicago/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Chicago</a> and <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/news/apple-adds-look-around-for-seattle-washington-in-maps/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Seattle</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/1C79D168-2035-4742-B0D8-35BE8A66C9A0-1597255094124.png\" alt=\"Look Around includes footpaths in Osaka, Japan.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Look Around includes footpaths in Osaka, Japan.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p9\">Now, Apple is expanding its rebuilt maps to other countries. Just this past week, the company added a detailed map of Japan, which includes the Look Around feature for Tokyo, Osaka, and Nagoya. In Osaka, Look Around &#8211; which had previously only followed streets &#8211; added footpaths in the park surrounding Osaka Castle, something I hope to see come to other cities too. Also coming later this year are rebuilt maps for the United Kingdom, Ireland, and Canada. It will be interesting to see if maps for those entire countries are turned on all at once, as was done with Japan. That may be difficult with a country the size of Canada, but I wouldn&#8217;t be surprised if that&#8217;s how Apple rolls out its new maps in the UK and Ireland.</p>\n<p id=\"p10\">New data layers are being added to Maps this year, including ones identifying speed and red-light cameras on your route, congestion zones, and environmental zones. Speed and red-light cameras have not appeared in the iOS 14 beta where I live yet, so I haven&#8217;t been able to try them. Presumably, however, they will work similarly to <a href=\"https://apps.apple.com/us/app/waze-navigation-live-traffic/id323229106\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">Google&#8217;s Waze app</a> by displaying iconography in Maps where cameras are located. Congestion and environmental zones data will help drivers take them into account when picking a route. Also, Maps users in China will be able to securely store their license plate numbers to check which days they are permitted to drive in congestion zones.</p>\n<h2 id=\"better-location-tracking-and-new-routing-options\">Better Location Tracking and New Routing Options</h2>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/43482c0c-43f2-47c3-a2f6-841b9e9cd134.png\" alt=\"Refine location in action.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Refine location in action.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p12\">The update to Maps adds an ingenious new way to determine your location too. Refine location is a clever way to pinpoint your location in a crowded city with tall buildings, which can reduce the accuracy of GPS. If iOS is unable to determine your location precisely, Maps will prompt you to hold up your phone and scan your surroundings with the iPhone&#8217;s camera. By comparing the images recorded against Look Around data and combining it with other available location data, Maps can more accurately place you on the map. Refine location is only available in <a href=\"https://www.justinobeirne.com/look-around-coverage-areas\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">cities with Look Around</a>, which is currently limited to the biggest US cities, plus Tokyo, Osaka, and Nagoya in Japan.</p>\n<p id=\"p13\">Maps will also add cycling and electric vehicle routing features this fall. When it launches, cycling routes will be available in San Francisco, New York, Shanghai, and Beijing, although currently the feature is only live in San Francisco.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/E7A5406E-D9B0-4391-A9FE-7747CEC4ACC1-1597258057066.png\" alt=\"Bicycle routing allows you to optimize for time or avoid hills.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Bicycle routing allows you to optimize for time or avoid hills.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p15\">Cycling routes include several options that let cyclists customize their trips. An excellent example of the feature is illustrated by a route I picked from Mint Plaza to In-N-Out Burger in San Francisco. After entering the start and end points of the trip, Maps displays a new bicycle icon that you can tap to see your route options.</p>\n<p id=\"p16\">In this example, Maps provides two options. The first one is the fastest at 23 minutes, but hillier than the second, which takes an extra minute of riding. Along with the distance and overall elevation change, Maps displays a graph that depicts the inclines along the way and notes other features of the route, like bike lanes, shared roads, and busy roads. Maps also alerts you if any walking or stair climbs may be necessary. The same details are displayed on the Apple Watch.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/8153180D-770F-4CE0-B93F-709B1CBBFFCB-1597258162334.png\" alt=\"You can also set Maps to avoid busy roads.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">You can also set Maps to avoid busy roads.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p18\">Beneath the default routes picked by Maps are options to avoid hills and busy roads. My imaginary ride to In-N-Out Burger is a good example of how these two toggles can affect a route. The most direct route down Stockton Street is hilly. Instructing Maps to avoid hills takes me on a longer, flatter route down Mission Street to the waterfront. Choosing to avoid busy roads suggests a different route to the Embarcadero via Market Street. The Maps section of the Settings app has also been updated to allow you to pick cycling as the default mode of transportation and to avoid busy roads and hills by default.</p>\n<p id=\"p19\">Maps is also adding electric vehicle routing. The feature hasn&#8217;t appeared in the iOS 14 beta yet, but Apple says it will allow users to specify their type of EV and supported charger. That will allow Apple to highlight charging stations at regular intervals along a route as needed and add the time needed to charge your vehicle when calculating your ETA to your destination.</p>\n<h2 id=\"built-in-third-party-city-guides\">Built-In Third-Party City Guides</h2>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/7CA9D6B5-F048-499B-960D-68D56FADBBF0-1597258213775.png\" alt=\"Guides can be found by searching for a place or topic like &#8216;ice cream,&#8217; for example.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Guides can be found by searching for a place or topic like &#8216;ice cream,&#8217; for example.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p21\">Another big addition coming to Maps is curated guides from third-party publishers, which can be located by searching for a city that supports them or for a topic covered by a guide. So far, I&#8217;ve seen guides from Lonely Planet, The Infatuation, and AllTrail, although between WWDC keynote slides and other reports, guides should also be coming from Louis Vuitton, The Washington Post, Time Out Group, Complex, and Zagat. Guides include collections of destinations like &#8216;Best Trails for Running Near SF&#8217; from AllTrails and &#8216;Best Ice Cream Shops in NYC&#8217; from Lonely Planet. Tapping on a guide drops pins for each location in the guide on the map and shows a short description of each location with accompanying photos. You can save an entire guide, share it via the share sheet, or save individual locations to custom collections you create.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/0B88960E-7899-4BCC-B7F5-7315D94B3F24-1597258213756.png\" alt=\"San Francisco and the surrounding area currently has the most guides, including outdoor activities, food, and tourist sites.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">San Francisco and the surrounding area currently has the most guides, including outdoor activities, food, and tourist sites.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p23\">The guides that I&#8217;ve browsed in San Francisco and New York are well done and concise, making them easy to digest. Currently, however, the catalog of guides is severely limited. At WWDC Apple said the feature will start with San Francisco, Los Angeles, New York, and London. As of the publication of this story, there are only three guides in New York, a couple dozen in the San Francisco area, and none in Los Angeles or London. So far, there are twelve guides from Lonely Planet, nine from The Infatuation, and five from AllTrails. There are still several weeks to go before iOS 14 launches, so I expect more guides to be added soon. However, as with other Maps features, it&#8217;s going to be a while before the feature is widespread enough to be useful to many users, but worth trying if you&#8217;re in San Francisco or New York.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/568B5FA5-7E54-4942-9871-99C687D27187-1597258213162.png\" alt=\"Guides are discoverable in search results and certain location listings, and in San Francisco you can browse all guides and browse by publisher.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Guides are discoverable in search results and certain location listings, and in San Francisco you can browse all guides and browse by publisher.</p></div>\n<h2 id=\"catching-up-the-mac-with-catalyst\">Catching Up the Mac with Catalyst</h2>\n<p id=\"p25\">Also notable this fall is that Maps will join the ranks of Mac Catalyst with the release of macOS Big Sur. Unlike some of Apple&#8217;s early Mac Catalyst offerings, Maps follows standard Mac conventions users expect, like using checkboxes in its Preferences instead of iOS-style toggles, and it fits in nicely with macOS&#8217;s new design language.</p>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/5fc562a9-8291-4217-bb7e-3a5175d46b45.png\" alt=\"Maps running on Big Sur.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">Maps running on Big Sur.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p27\">Beyond its looks, though, Maps being rebuilt as a Mac Catalyst app means it now enjoys feature parity with the iOS and iPadOS versions of the app. For the first time, Maps on the Mac supports Favorites, Look Around, and indoor maps. You can also send routes to an iPhone or iPad and track arrival times for anyone who has shared their ETA with you. Maps on the Mac will support the new iOS and iPadOS 14 features that I covered above too. With a full sidebar on the left and toolbar across the top of the app&#8217;s window, Maps on macOS 11.0 remains unmistakably a Mac app, but with all the features users will expect.</p>\n<h2 id=\"approximate-location-provides-more-privacy-options\">Approximate Location Provides More Privacy Options</h2>\n<div class=\"media-wrapper\"><img src=\"https://2672686a4cf38e8c2458-2712e00ea34e3076747650c92426bbb5.ssl.cf1.rackcdn.com/532E24C5-B556-43AE-A70F-663C4AFAED80-1597258211596.png\" alt=\"If you only share your approximate location with Maps, your location is depicted by a large shaded circle.\"><p class=\"image-caption\">If you only share your approximate location with Maps, your location is depicted by a large shaded circle.</p></div>\n<p id=\"p29\">Privacy has also been enhanced in Maps to account for the fact that Apple&#8217;s CoreLocation APIs now allow users to share their approximate location with apps instead of their precise location. The change posed difficult design challenges for Maps, which was built around the assumption that it would always know a user&#8217;s precise location. The solution, which is <a href=\"https://developer.apple.com/wwdc20/10162\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">detailed in a WWDC presentation</a>, implements a combination of user control, clear in-app communication, and an explanation of the value of providing precise data to allow users to make an informed choice while remaining useful to users who only provide approximate data.</p>\n<p id=\"p30\">The most apparent result of the change is that if you only grant Maps access to your approximate location, your position on the map is represented by a shaded circle encompassing your approximate location instead of a precise blue dot. The addition of approximate location sharing is a welcome one. Although I plan to continue sharing my exact location with Maps to take full advantage of things like turn-by-turn directions, there are plenty of other apps, like ones that list upcoming local events, that only need my general location.</p>\n<hr id=\"hr31\"><p id=\"p32\">When <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/stories/apple-maps-in-ios-13-sights-set-on-google/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">we checked in with Maps after WWDC 2019</a>, a large part of the US still did not have Apple&#8217;s enhanced maps, and Look Around was just rolling out in the San Francisco area. Now, the entire US and Japan have Apple&#8217;s new maps, and Canada, the UK, and Ireland are on the way. Plus, eleven US cities and three Japanese cities now feature Look Around. The progress is substantial, but there is still a long way to go before the new maps and Look Around are available worldwide.</p>\n<p id=\"p33\">Apple isn&#8217;t sitting still, though. While other countries are being mapped and brought online, new features are being built on top of the new maps where they are already available, which I expect will continue. It&#8217;s a frustratingly slow process at times, but given the size of the undertaking, it&#8217;s not surprising. In fact, what has been accomplished so far is actually quite remarkable. Apple hasn&#8217;t shared its plans for mapping beyond the countries that will be added later this year, but if the progress made in the US is any indication, we&#8217;ll see many more countries get Apple&#8217;s new maps and features between now and WWDC 2021.</p>\n<hr id=\"hr34\"><p id=\"p35\"><em>You can also follow our 2020 Summer OS Preview Series through our <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/tag/os-preview-2020/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">dedicated hub</a>, or <a href=\"https://www.macstories.net/tag/os-preview-2020/feed/\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\">subscribe to its RSS feed</a>.</em></p>\n<hr /><h3>Support MacStories Directly</h3><p><p>Club MacStories offers exclusive access to <strong>extra MacStories content</strong>, delivered every week;  it&#8217;s also a way to support us directly.</p>\n<p>Club MacStories will help you discover the best apps for your devices and get the most out of your iPhone, iPad, and Mac.
